<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41588"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="High-quality de novo assembly of the apple genome and methylome dynamics of early fruit development"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Genetics 2017 49:7"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2017-06-05"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2017 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Etienne Bucher and colleagues use a combination of short- and long-read sequencing, along with optical mapping technologies, to produce the high-quality de novo assembly of the apple genome. They identify a new repetitive retrotransposon sequence and analyze DNA methylation data in relation to important agronomic traits. Using the latest sequencing and optical mapping technologies, we have produced a high-quality de novo assembly of the apple (Malus domestica Borkh.) genome. Repeat sequences, which represented over half of the assembly, provided an unprecedented opportunity to investigate the uncharacterized regions of a tree genome; we identified a new hyper-repetitive retrotransposon sequence that was over-represented in heterochromatic regions and estimated that a major burst of different transposable elements (TEs) occurred 21 million years ago. Notably, the timing of this TE burst coincided with the uplift of the Tian Shan mountains, which is thought to be the center of the location where the apple originated, suggesting that TEs and associated processes may have contributed to the diversification of the apple ancestor and possibly to its divergence from pear. Finally, genome-wide DNA methylation data suggest that epigenetic marks may contribute to agronomically relevant aspects, such as apple fruit development."/>

            </div>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs2" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs2">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs2-content"><p>Using the latest sequencing and optical mapping technologies, we have produced a high-quality <i>de novo</i> assembly of the apple (<i>Malus domestica</i> Borkh.) genome. Repeat sequences, which represented over half of the assembly, provided an unprecedented opportunity to investigate the uncharacterized regions of a tree genome; we identified a new hyper-repetitive retrotransposon sequence that was over-represented in heterochromatic regions and estimated that a major burst of different transposable elements (TEs) occurred 21 million years ago. Notably, the timing of this TE burst coincided with the uplift of the Tian Shan mountains, which is thought to be the center of the location where the apple originated, suggesting that TEs and associated processes may have contributed to the diversification of the apple ancestor and possibly to its divergence from pear. Finally, genome-wide DNA methylation data suggest that epigenetic marks may contribute to agronomically relevant aspects, such as apple fruit development.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Accurate sequence information, genome assemblies and annotations are the foundation for genetic and genome-wide studies. The major factors that limit <i>de novo</i> genome assembly are heterozygosity and repetitive sequences, such as TEs, which are often collapsed to single copies in draft genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Veeckman, E., Ruttink, T. &amp; Vandepoele, K. Are we there yet? Reliably estimating the completeness of plant genome sequences. Plant Cell 28, 1759–1768 (2016)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d68502e791">1</a></sup>. In recent years, however, evidence supporting the importance of TEs in genome evolution, genome structure, regulation of gene expression and epigenetics has been mounting<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="ENCODE Project Consortium. An integrated encyclopedia of DNA elements in the human genome. Nature 489, 57–74 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d68502e795">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Fedoroff, N.V. Transposable elements, epigenetics and genome evolution. Science 338, 758–767 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d68502e798">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Chénais, B., Caruso, A., Hiard, S. &amp; Casse, N. The impact of transposable elements on eukaryotic genomes: from genome size increase to genetic adaptation to stressful environments. Gene 509, 7–15 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d68502e801">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Lisch, D. How important are transposons for plant evolution? Nat. Rev. Genet. 14, 49–61 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d68502e804">5</a></sup>. The characterization of sequences and the distribution of TEs within a genome is, therefore, of great importance.</p><p>Until now, the study of epigenetically controlled characteristics in perennial plants has been hampered by the draft status of their genome sequences. In the case of apple, a draft was produced<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Velasco, R. et al. The genome of the domesticated apple (Malus × domestica Borkh.). Nat. Genet. 42, 833–839 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d68502e811">6</a></sup> but remained incomplete with inaccurate contig positions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Khan, M.A., Han, Y., Zhao, Y.F., Troggio, M. &amp; Korban, S.S. A multi-population consensus genetic map reveals inconsistent marker order among maps likely attributed to structural variations in the apple genome. PLoS One 7, e47864 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d68502e815">7</a></sup>; this hindered its utility for genetic and epigenetic studies. <i>De novo</i> sequencing and assembly of a new genome for apple, using technologies of the third generation, had thus become a necessity.</p><p>In the last few years, single-molecule sequencing and optical-mapping technologies have emerged<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Ansorge, W.J. Next-generation DNA sequencing (II): techniques, applications. Next Generat. Sequenc. &amp; Applic. 1, 1–10 (2016)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d68502e825">8</a></sup>, which are well suited for assembling genomic regions that contain long repetitive elements. Recently, several high-quality genome assemblies have been published using one or both technologies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Zhang, G. et al. Hybrid de novo genome assembly of the Chinese herbal plant danshen (Salvia miltiorrhiza Bunge). Gigascience 4, 62 (2015)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d68502e829">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="VanBuren, R. et al. Single-molecule sequencing of the desiccation-tolerant grass Oropetium thomaeum. Nature 527, 508–511 (2015)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d68502e832">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Zapata, L. et al. Chromosome-level assembly of Arabidopsis thaliana Ler reveals the extent of translocation and inversion polymorphisms. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 113, E4052–E4060 (2016)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d68502e835">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Redwan, R.M., Saidin, A. &amp; Kumar, S.V. The draft genome of MD-2 pineapple using hybrid error correction of long reads. DNA Res. 23, 427–439 (2016)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d68502e838">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Mahesh, H.B. et al. Indica rice genome assembly, annotation and mining of blast-disease-resistance genes. BMC Genomics 17, 242 (2016)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d68502e841">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Badouin, H. et al. Chaos of rearrangements in the mating-type chromosomes of the anther-smut fungus Microbotryum lychnidis-dioicae. Genetics 200, 1275–1284 (2015)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d68502e844">14</a></sup>. The use of long-read sequencing technologies may also tackle potential assembly issues that are related to the presence of highly similar sequences resulting from whole-genome duplication events that frequently occurred in angiosperm genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Cui, L. et al. Widespread genome duplications throughout the history of flowering plants. Genome Res. 16, 738–749 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d68502e848">15</a></sup>.</p><p>In addition to DNA sequence modifications, it has been shown that epigenetic variations contribute to genome accessibility, functionality and structure<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Roudier, F., Teixeira, F.K. &amp; Colot, V. Chromatin indexing in Arabidopsis: an epigenomic tale of tails and more. Trends Genet. 25, 511–517 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d68502e855">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="He, G., Elling, A.A. &amp; Deng, X.W. The epigenome and plant development. Annu. Rev. Plant Biol. 62, 411–435 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d68502e858">17</a></sup>. Several studies have demonstrated that local DNA methylation variants, which are represented by differential cytosine methylation at particular loci, can have major effects on the transcription of nearby genes and can be inherited over generations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Cubas, P., Vincent, C. &amp; Coen, E. An epigenetic mutation responsible for natural variation in floral symmetry. Nature 401, 157–161 (1999)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d68502e862">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Becker, C. et al. Spontaneous epigenetic variation in the Arabidopsis thaliana methylome. Nature 480, 245–249 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d68502e865">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Ong-Abdullah, M. et al. Loss of Karma transposon methylation underlies the mantled somaclonal variant of oil palm. Nature 525, 533–537 (2015)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d68502e868">20</a></sup>.</p><p>Apple, like most other fruit tree crops, is propagated by grafting onto rootstocks, which over time can allow the acquisition and propagation of epimutations, via variation in DNA methylation states that can influence various phenotypes, such as fruit color<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="El-Sharkawy, I., Liang, D. &amp; Xu, K. Transcriptome analysis of an apple (Malus × domestica) yellow fruit somatic mutation identifies a gene network module highly associated with anthocyanin and epigenetic regulation. J. Exp. Bot. 66, 7359–7376 (2015)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d68502e876">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Telias, A. et al. Apple skin patterning is associated with differential expression of MYB10. BMC Plant Biol. 11, 93 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d68502e879">22</a></sup>. Thus, knowledge of the epigenetic landscape of apple cultivars could provide new tools to study somatic variants, leading to the development of epigenetic markers for marker-assisted selection.</p><p>To produce a high-quality apple reference genome and methylome, we generated a <i>de novo</i> assembly of a 'Golden Delicious' doubled-haploid tree (GDDH13) composed of 280 assembled scaffolds and arranged into 17 pseudomolecules, which represent the 17 chromosomes of apple. This assembly resulted from a combination of short (Illumina) and long sequencing reads (PacBio), along with scaffolding based on optical maps (BioNano) and a high-density integrated genetic linkage map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Di Pierro, E.A. et al. A high-density, multi-parental SNP genetic map on apple validates a new mapping approach for outcrossing species. Hortic. Res. 3, 16057 (2016)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d68502e889">23</a></sup>. This chromosome-scale assembly was complemented by a detailed <i>de novo</i> annotation of genes based on RNA sequencing (RNA-seq) data, TE annotation and small RNA alignments.</p><p>To understand the potential role of epigenetic marks on fruit development, we constructed genome-wide DNA methylation maps that compared different tissues and two isogenic apple lines that produce large or small fruits. This led to the identification of differential DNA methylation patterns that are associated with genes involved in fruit development.</p><p>This work provides a solid foundation for future genetic and epigenomic studies in apple. Furthermore, our TE annotation provides novel insights into the evolutionary history of apple and may contribute to explaining its divergence from pear.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome sequencing, assembly and scaffolding</h3><p>The doubled-haploid Golden Delicious line (GDDH13, also coded X9273) used in this study is the result of breeding efforts that were initiated at INRA in 1963 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Lespinasse, Y., Bouvier, L., Djulbic, M. &amp; Chevreau, E. Haploidy in apple and pear. Acta Hortic. 484, 49–54 (1998)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d68502e913">24</a>) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#Fig6">Supplementary Fig. 1</a> and Online <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#Sec11">Methods</a>). Homozygosity of this line was confirmed with microsatellite markers that are distributed along the apple genome (data not shown) and by observation of the k-mer spectrum of Illumina reads derived from GDDH13 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Assembly and validation of the GDDH13 doubled-haploid apple genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: Assembly and validation of the GDDH13 doubled-haploid apple genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3886/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3886/MediaObjects/41588_2017_Article_BFng3886_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3886/MediaObjects/41588_2017_Article_BFng3886_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="299"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) k-mer (23 bp) spectra of the doubled-haploid GDDH13 and the heterozygous Golden Delicious<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Li, X. et al. Improved hybrid de novo genome assembly of domesticated apple (Malus × domestica). Gigascience 5, 35 (2016)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d68502e945">33</a></sup> genomes. The <i>x</i> axis represents k-mer multiplicity, and the <i>y</i> axis represents the number of k-mers with a given multiplicity in the sequencing data. The green dashed line represents the ideal Poisson distribution fitted on the data of GDDH13. (<b>b</b>) Overview of the processing pipeline used for the assembly of the GDDH13 genome (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a> for details). (<b>c</b>) Graphical representation of the location of SNP markers on the physical map (<i>x</i> axis), as compared to their position on the integrated genetic map (<i>y</i> axis), for Chr11 of the GDDH13 genome. Each marker is depicted as a circle on the plot (1,069 data points). The colors depict the chromosomes as follows: red for Chr01, light green for Chr04, pink for Chr08, blue for Chr10 and violet for Chr11. (<b>d</b>) Graphical representation of the mean local recombination rates between successive SNP markers along Chr11 (3-Mb sliding window, 1-Mb shift, threshold 4). The <i>x</i> axis represents the physical positions of the means on Chr11, and the <i>y</i> axis indicates the recombination ratio (centiMorgan (cM)/Mb) in each 3-Mb sliding window. (<b>e</b>) Heat map of genotypic linkage disequilibrium (LD; <i>r</i><sup>2</sup>) in Chr11 in the 'Old Dessert' INRA apple core collection. Shown are the graphical representation of the location of SNPs on the physical map (top) with correspondence to their order in a regular distribution (bottom) of Chr11 (1,461,195 data points). The color bar indicates the level of LD, from high LD (red) and low LD (blue).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3886/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To perform <i>de novo</i> assembly of the GDDH13 genome, we combined three different technologies: short-read sequencing, long-read sequencing and optical mapping (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#Fig1">Fig. 1b</a>). Using DNA from the leaves of GDDH13, we generated <span class="stix">∼</span>120-fold coverage of Illumina paired-end reads (72 Gb), 80-fold coverage of Illumina Nextera mate-pair reads (58 Gb) at three different insert sizes (2, 5 and 10 kb) and <span class="stix">∼</span>35-fold coverage of PacBio sequencing data (24 Gb; 2,837,045 subreads with a mean length of 8,474 bp). The Illumina paired-end reads were first assembled using SOAPdenovo<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience 1, 18 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d68502e1005">25</a></sup>, and the resulting contigs were combined with the PacBio reads using the DBG2OLC assembler<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Ye, C., Hill, C.M., Wu, S., Ruan, J. &amp; Ma, Z.S. DBG2OLC: efficient assembly of large genomes using long erroneous reads of the third-generation sequencing technologies. Sci. Rep. 6, 31900 (2016)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d68502e1009">26</a></sup>. This resulted in an assembly that consisted of 2,150 contigs with an N50 of 620 kb (i.e., 50% of the assembly was contained in contigs ≥620 kb in size) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 1</a>) and a total length of 625.2 Mb, which were subsequently corrected by using the Illumina paired-end reads (94,896 single-base assembly errors corrected; 1,054,709 insertions (1,466,015 bp) and 123,510 deletions (178,733 bp)) and scaffolded by using Illumina mate-pair reads with BESST (assembly N50 increased from 620 kb to 699 kb).</p><p>Next, using a <span class="stix">∼</span>600-fold-coverage BioNano optical map, we generated a consensus map that resulted in an assembly of 649.7 Mb. This consensus map was then used for the hybrid assembly with the corrected scaffolds, which, together with single-nucleotide polymorphism (SNP) markers derived from a high-density genetic linkage map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Di Pierro, E.A. et al. A high-density, multi-parental SNP genetic map on apple validates a new mapping approach for outcrossing species. Hortic. Res. 3, 16057 (2016)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d68502e1019">23</a></sup>, allowed the construction of the 17 pseudochromosomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>). To estimate the genome size, we calculated different k-mer frequency distributions of the Illumina reads. The estimated GDDH13 genome size of 651 Mb was very close to the 649.7-Mb size in the consensus map.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Assessment of genome quality</h3><p>We assessed the quality of the assembly by using two independent sources of data. First, we used the SNP markers that were mapped on the previously mentioned integrated genetic linkage map to validate scaffold assembly. Of the 15,417 SNP probe sequences, we identified sequence homology in the GDDH13 genome for 14,732 of them. We then assessed their position on the scaffold assemblies by comparing their location on the integrated genetic linkage map. In total 14,117 of the mapped markers (95.8%) were found to be located at their expected positions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>). To visualize these data, we plotted the genetic distance against the physical distance of the genetic markers for each chromosome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#Fig7">Supplementary Fig. 2</a>); the data for chromosome (Chr) 11 is shown as an example in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#Fig1">Figure 1c</a>. This analysis showed that there was very little discrepancy between the physical and genetic maps. For comparison, we plotted these markers to the heterozygous apple genome (v 1.0; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#Fig8">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>). We also plotted the recombination rates in sliding windows of 3 Mb on this chromosome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#Fig1">Fig. 1d</a>) and identified a decrease in recombination frequency toward the middle of Chr11.</p><p>Second, we estimated the level of linkage disequilibrium (LD) using the <i>r</i><sup>2</sup> parameter between all pairwise SNP comparisons by using marker data that were derived from an apple core collection<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Lassois, L. et al. Genetic diversity, population structure, parentage analysis and construction of core collections in the French apple germplasm based on SSR markers. Plant Mol. Biol. Rep. 34, 827–844 (2016)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d68502e1059">27</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Bianco, L. et al. Development and validation of the Axiom Apple480K SNP genotyping array. Plant J. 86, 62–74 (2016)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d68502e1062">28</a></sup>. In the present version of the GDDH13 genome, we did not identify any abrupt jumps in LD, indicating the overall robustness of the assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#Fig1">Fig. 1e</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#Fig9">Supplementary Fig. 4</a>). Using previously published genetic data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Falginella, L. et al. A major QTL controlling apple skin russeting maps on the linkage group 12 of 'Renetta Grigia di Torriana'. BMC Plant Biol. 15, 150 (2015)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d68502e1072">29</a></sup>, we generated a haplotype map for GDDH13, which allowed the identification of recombination breakpoints (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#Fig10">Supplementary Fig. 5</a>).</p><p>Finally, the completeness of the assembly was tested by searching for 248 core eukaryotic genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Parra, G., Bradnam, K. &amp; Korf, I. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics 23, 1061–1067 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d68502e1083">30</a></sup> (CEGs). In total, 237 of 248 CEGs were completely present, and 7 CEGs were partially present, indicating that fewer than 2% of the CEGs could not be detected, which compared very favorably with other assemblies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Bradnam, K.R. et al. Assemblathon 2: evaluating de novo methods of genome assembly in three vertebrate species. Gigascience 2, 10 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d68502e1087">31</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Genome annotation</h3><p>To obtain a global view of the apple transcriptome, we performed a high-throughput RNA-seq analysis on poly(A)-enriched RNAs from nine libraries that originated from different genotypes and tissues. RNA-seq reads were assembled, and the resulting contigs were mapped to the scaffolds and integrated in the EuGene combiner pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Foissac, S. et al. Genome annotation in plants and fungi: EuGene as a model platform. Curr. Bioinform. 3, 87–97 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d68502e1099">32</a></sup>. In total, we identified 42,140 protein-coding genes (which represent 23.3% of the genome assembly) and 1,965 non-protein-coding genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>). Evidence of transcription was found for 93% of the annotated genes.</p><p>To further evaluate the quality of the annotation, a comparison with annotations of previous apple genome assemblies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Velasco, R. et al. The genome of the domesticated apple (Malus × domestica Borkh.). Nat. Genet. 42, 833–839 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d68502e1112">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Li, X. et al. Improved hybrid de novo genome assembly of domesticated apple (Malus × domestica). Gigascience 5, 35 (2016)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d68502e1115">33</a></sup> was performed using the BUSCO v2 method, which is based on a benchmark of 1,440 conserved plant genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Simão, F.A., Waterhouse, R.M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E.V. &amp; Zdobnov, E.M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210–3212 (2015)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d68502e1119">34</a></sup>. The results indicate that our apple genome annotation is the most complete, despite having the lowest number of predicted genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#Tab1">Table 1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Comparison of the GDDH13 genome with previously published assemblies of the apple genome</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ng.3886/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The <i>de novo</i> annotated genes were named using the following convention: <i>MD</i> (for <i>Malus domestica</i>) followed by the chromosome number and gene number on the chromosome (in steps of 100) going from top to bottom according to the linkage map, for example, <i>MD13G0052100</i>.</p><p>Previously published small RNA (sRNA) data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Celton, J.M. et al. Widespread antisense transcription in apple is correlated with siRNA production and indicates a large potential for transcriptional and/or post-transcriptional control. New Phytol. 203, 287–299 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d68502e1406">35</a></sup> were also mapped to the genome. We found that most 21- and 22-nt-long sRNAs mapped to protein-coding genes, whereas most 24-nt-long sRNAs mapped to TEs. The distribution of 23-nt-long sRNAs was evenly included in both types of genomic features (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#Fig11">Supplementary Fig. 6</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Ancestral genome duplication</h3><p>Intragenomic synteny of GDDH13 was assessed using SynMap (CoGe; <a href="http://www.genomevolution.org">http://www.genomevolution.org</a>) and visualized with Circos<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Krzywinski, M. et al. Circos: an information aesthetic for comparative genomics. Genome Res. 19, 1639–1645 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d68502e1428">36</a></sup>. Results of this analysis (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>) showed an even clearer genome duplication pattern than has previously been reported<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Velasco, R. et al. The genome of the domesticated apple (Malus × domestica Borkh.). Nat. Genet. 42, 833–839 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d68502e1435">6</a></sup>. Only very few regions showed no synteny to other parts of the genome (for example, the middle part of Chr04).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Synteny and distribution of genomic and epigenomic features of the apple genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Synteny and distribution of genomic and epigenomic features of the apple genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3886/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3886/MediaObjects/41588_2017_Article_BFng3886_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3886/MediaObjects/41588_2017_Article_BFng3886_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="497"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>The rings indicate (from outside to inside, as indicated in the inset) chromosomes (Chr), heat maps representing gene density (green), TE density (blue) and DNA methylation levels (orange). A map connecting homologous regions of the apple genome is shown inside the figure. The colored lines link collinearity blocks that represent syntenic regions that were identified by SynMap.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3886/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Transposable elements and annotation of repeat sequences</h3><p>To produce a genome-wide annotation of repetitive sequences, TE consensus sequences (provided by the TE<i>denovo</i> detection pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Flutre, T., Duprat, E., Feuillet, C. &amp; Quesneville, H. Considering transposable element diversification in de novo annotation approaches. PLoS One 6, e16526 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d68502e1470">37</a></sup>) were used to annotate their copies in the whole genome. To refine this annotation, we performed two iterations of the TEannot pipeline. In the GDDH13 genome, TEs represented 372.2 Mb (57.3% of the 649.7 Mb BioNano assembly; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 2</a>). Excluding undefined bases (Ns), the TE content of the total nucleotide space in the final annotation was 59.5% of the assembly. The most abundant repeats in this genome are retrotransposons or class I elements (74.8% of TE content, 42.9% of genome assembly), and in particular long terminal repeat retrotransposons (LTR-RTs), which represent 66% of this type of repeat, whereas non-LTR retrotransposons (LINE and SINE) accounted for 7% (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 2</a>). DNA transposons or class II elements (DNA transposons and Helitrons) make up 23% of the TE content (13.4% of the genome assembly; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 2</a>). A complete list of identified TEs, their integrity and copy number can be found in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#MOESM2">Supplementary Table 3</a>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Distribution and evolution of transposable elements in the apple genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Distribution and evolution of transposable elements in the apple genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3886/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3886/MediaObjects/41588_2017_Article_BFng3886_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3886/MediaObjects/41588_2017_Article_BFng3886_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="482"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Percentage of base pairs of the assembled GDDH13 genome that represent genes, pseudo-genes, TEs and non-annotated regions. Retrotransposons (class I) are shown in shades of red, and DNA transposons (class II) are shown in shades of blue. (<b>b</b>) Chromosomal density plots of all TE families on Chr01 to Chr04 (top), and the recombination rate for each corresponding chromosome (3-Mb sliding window) (bottom). (<b>c</b>) Distribution of sequence identity values between genomic copies and consensus repeats in the GDDH13 assembly (based on 2,198,722 data points). The relative frequencies per percentage of identity of the Helitron, TIR, LTR, LINE, SINE and unclassified TEs (NoCat) are represented in different colors.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3886/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We ran the REPET<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Quesneville, H. et al. Combined evidence annotation of transposable elements in genome sequences. PLOS Comput. Biol. 1, 166–175 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d68502e1525">38</a></sup> pipeline on the PacBio contigs, which allowed us to identify an additional hyper-repetitive consensus sequence (Genbank entry <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=KX869746">KX869746</a>). This consensus sequence was automatically classified as a 9,716-bp LTR-RT with over 500 full-length copies, and it accounted for 3.6% of the genome assembly (22.3 Mb). We termed this TE consensus sequence <i>HODOR</i> (high-copy Golden Delicious repeat). At the chromosomal level, a higher density of <i>HODOR</i> copies coincided with particular regions of each chromosome that show reduced recombination levels, whereas the density level of other TEs remained constant or was decreased at these same regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#Fig12">Supplementary Fig. 7</a>). Even though the retrotransposon consensus sequence has clear 5′ and 3′ LTRs that are 1.8 kb in size, there are no homologies with typical TE-related sequences encoding a gag protein, a reverse transcriptase or an integrase. However, we found partial sequence similarity to the <i>Malus domestica</i> Copia-100 element present in RepBase Update<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Jurka, J. et al. Repbase Update, a database of eukaryotic repetitive elements. Cytogenet. Genome Res. 110, 462–467 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d68502e1552">39</a></sup>, corresponding to different domains such as gag pre-integrase, RNase H and integrase. These results suggest that <i>HODOR</i> is a non-autonomous LTR retrotransposon derivative or LARD (large retrotransposon derivative). We scanned the genome and were able to identify TEs that could contribute to the mobilization of <i>HODOR</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#MOESM2">Supplementary Table 3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>). Notably, we also found significant (BLASTX <i>e</i>-values ≤ 1 × 10<sup>−29</sup>) similarities with sequences encoding three short bacterial proteins of unknown function (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#Fig13">Supplementary Fig. 8a</a>), and mining of transcriptome data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Celton, J.M. et al. Widespread antisense transcription in apple is correlated with siRNA production and indicates a large potential for transcriptional and/or post-transcriptional control. New Phytol. 203, 287–299 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d68502e1577">35</a></sup> showed <i>HODOR</i> to be primarily transcribed in the sense and antisense orientations in apple seeds (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#Fig13">Supplementary Fig. 8b</a>).</p><p>To investigate the evolutionary history of TEs in the apple genome, we plotted the distribution of identity values between genomic copies and their consensus sequences (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#Fig3">Fig. 3c</a>). Distributions for all classes of repeats showed a peak at 77% identity. By considering the mutation rate that has been reported for LTR-RTs in plants (1.3 × 10<sup>−8</sup> base substitutions per site per year<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Ma, J. &amp; Bennetzen, J.L. Recombination, rearrangement, reshuffling and divergence in a centromeric region of rice. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 103, 383–388 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d68502e1596">40</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Yin, H. et al. Genome-wide annotation and comparative analysis of long-terminal-repeat retrotransposons between pear species of P. bretschneideri and P. communis. Sci. Rep. 5, 17644 (2015)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d68502e1599">41</a></sup>), we estimated the age of those insertions as described by the International Human Genome Sequencing Consortium<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Lander, E.S. et al. Initial sequencing and analysis of the human genome. Nature 409, 860–921 (2001)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d68502e1603">42</a></sup>. We concluded that the peak at 77% identity corresponded to an insertion age of around 21 million years ago (Mya) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#Fig3">Fig. 3c</a>). We also noted a second peak, particularly for LINE elements, at 98% identity that corresponded to a TE burst at <span class="stix">∼</span>1.6 Mya (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#Fig3">Fig. 3c</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">The apple methylome</h3><p>To investigate the apple methylome, we produced genome-wide maps of DNA methylation content at single-base resolution for GDDH13 leaves and young fruits<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Lister, R. et al. Highly integrated single-base resolution maps of the epigenome in Arabidopsis. Cell 133, 523–536 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d68502e1622">43</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Cokus, S.J. et al. Shotgun bisulphite sequencing of the Arabidopsis genome reveals DNA methylation patterning. Nature 452, 215–219 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d68502e1625">44</a></sup>.</p><p>Globally, in leaves we found DNA methylation levels of 49%, 39% and 12% in the CG, CHG and CHH sequence contexts (where H is adenine, thymine or cytosine), respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#Fig4">Fig. 4a</a>). DNA methylation was not evenly spread throughout the chromosomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#Fig4">Fig. 4b</a> shows the profile for Chr11; see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#Fig14">Supplementary Fig. 9</a> for the profiles for all of the chromosomes), and peaks of methylation coincided with recombination cold spots.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="DNA methylation landscape of the GDDH13 genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: DNA methylation landscape of the GDDH13 genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3886/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3886/MediaObjects/41588_2017_Article_BFng3886_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3886/MediaObjects/41588_2017_Article_BFng3886_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="471"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Percentage of DNA methylation distributions of the three methylation contexts (CG, CHG or CHH) in <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Cokus, S.J. et al. Shotgun bisulphite sequencing of the Arabidopsis genome reveals DNA methylation patterning. Nature 452, 215–219 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d68502e1658">44</a></sup>, soybean<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Schmitz, R.J. et al. Epigenome-wide inheritance of cytosine methylation variants in a recombinant inbred population. Genome Res. 23, 1663–1674 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d68502e1662">60</a></sup> and apple. For apple, the percentages were estimated based on the number of cytosines that had a methylation ratio ≥0.75. (<b>b</b>) Top, chromosomal distribution of the methylation ratios along Chr11. Bottom, the recombination rate plot from <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#Fig1">Figure 1d</a>, for comparison purposes. (<b>c</b>) Global distribution of DNA methylation levels at protein-coding genes, TEs and <i>HODOR</i>, including a 1-kb window upstream of the TSS and downstream of the transcription end site (TES). In all of the panels, the DNA methylation sequence contexts are color-coded as follows: brown for CG, yellow for CHG and blue for CHH.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3886/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>As expected<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Matzke, M.A. &amp; Mosher, R.A. RNA-directed DNA methylation: an epigenetic pathway of increasing complexity. Nat. Rev. Genet. 15, 394–408 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d68502e1690">45</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Law, J.A. &amp; Jacobsen, S.E. Establishing, maintaining and modifying DNA methylation patterns in plants and animals. Nat. Rev. Genet. 11, 204–220 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d68502e1693">46</a></sup>, there are reduced overall DNA methylation levels in gene sequences, whereas TEs are extensively methylated (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#Fig4">Fig. 4c</a>). For genes, we identified three major types of DNA methylation patterns. Genes in cluster 1 were characterized by high levels of DNA methylation in the gene body in the CG and CHG contexts, which was concomitant with high DNA methylation in the surrounding regions. Genes in cluster 2 had low CG, and very low CHG and CHH, methylation in the gene itself, yet there were increased levels in the surrounding region. Finally, genes in cluster 3 featured low DNA methylation levels in both the gene body and in the surrounding regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#Fig15">Supplementary Fig. 10</a>). This last cluster contained the largest number of genes (27,179; 64.5% of all genes), showing that in apple, genes are generally depleted for DNA methylation. By mining previously produced large transcriptome data sets for apple<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Celton, J.M. et al. Widespread antisense transcription in apple is correlated with siRNA production and indicates a large potential for transcriptional and/or post-transcriptional control. New Phytol. 203, 287–299 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d68502e1703">35</a></sup>, we found that genes covered with very high levels of DNA methylation (cluster 1) showed the lowest expression levels (1.58 median log<sub>2</sub> value), whereas cluster 2 and cluster 3 genes had higher log<sub>2</sub> values (3.3 and 2.8, respectively). This result confirmed that the amount of DNA methylation surrounding genes influences their expression level. As one example of TEs, we assessed the DNA methylation levels for <i>HODOR</i> and found that <i>HODOR</i> was almost completely methylated in the CG (90% methylated) and CHG (65% methylated) contexts but that it had much less methylation in the CHH context (3%) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#Fig4">Fig. 4c</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">DNA methylation and fruit development</h3><p>To assess how DNA methylation contributes to fruit development, we first compared DNA methylation levels between leaves and fruits. We called differentially methylated regions (DMRs) using a hidden Markov model (HMM)-based approach<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Hagmann, J. et al. Century-scale methylome stability in a recently diverged Arabidopsis thaliana lineage. PLoS Genet. 11, e1004920 (2015)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d68502e1729">47</a></sup>. In total, we identified 1,017 high-confidence DMRs in all contexts between leaves and fruits, and we observed a very strong bias for DMRs containing methylation changes in the CHH context (875 DMRs; 86.0%) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#Fig5">Fig. 5a</a>). We identified 294 genes that contained DMRs in their promoter region—14 DMRs were in the CHG context and showed increased amounts of DNA methylation in leaves, whereas the remaining 280 DMRs were found in the CHH context and showed increased amounts of DNA methylation in fruits. Thus, most methylation differences between leaves and fruits occurred at CHH sites, with a robust increase observed in the developing fruit. Among genes with DMRs that were 2 kb upstream of their transcription start site (TSS), we identified several apple orthologs of <i>Arabidopsis</i> genes with important roles in flower and fruit development and in epigenetic regulation (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#Fig5">Fig. 5b</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Differentially methylated regions between apple tree leaves and young fruits."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 5: Differentially methylated regions between apple tree leaves and young fruits.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3886/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3886/MediaObjects/41588_2017_Article_BFng3886_Fig5_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3886/MediaObjects/41588_2017_Article_BFng3886_Fig5_HTML.jpg" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="361"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) DMR content in samples of GDDH13 leaves and young fruits (CHH, <i>n</i> = 875 DMRs; CHG, <i>n</i> = 88 DMRs; CG, <i>n</i> = 21 DMRs; CG and CHG, <i>n</i> = 17 DMRs; CHG and CHH, <i>n</i> = 14 DMRs; CG and CHH, <i>n</i> = 2 DMRs). Most of the DMRs (86%) were identified in the CHH context. (<b>b</b>) Selection of GDDH13 genes that present a DMR within a region 2 kb upstream of the TSS. The apple gene ID, the methylation context of the DMR, the orthologous <i>Arabidopsis</i> gene annotation and the function of the encoded protein are listed. (<b>c</b>,<b>d</b>) Representative image comparing the fruit sizes of heterozygous Golden Delicious, GDDH13 and GDDH18 at harvest (<b>c</b>) and quantification of the number of cell layers in the parenchyma of GDDH13 (orange) and GDDH18 (green) fruits, as assessed by microscopy (<i>n</i> = 12 data points per box plot) (<b>d</b>). The horizontal line in the box represents the median, the lower and upper hinges correspond to the first and third quartiles, the lower and upper whiskers extend from the hinge to the smallest and largest value (no further than 1.5-fold the inter-quartile range from the hinge), and outlying points are plotted individually. Scale bar, 1 cm.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3886/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Next we wanted to test whether DNA methylation could have a role in the regulation of fruit size. We took advantage of GDDH18, an isogenic line that was obtained from the same haploid that produced GDDH13 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>). Whole-genome sequencing showed the presence of 27 homozygous SNPs within genes between the two trees, with nine of these SNPs resulting in amino acid changes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#MOESM3">Supplementary Table 4</a>). Although the GDDH13 and GDDH18 trees were indistinguishable, the GDDH18 fruits were much smaller (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#Fig5">Fig. 5c</a>) because of a reduced number of cell layers in the parenchyma (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#Fig5">Fig. 5d</a>).</p><p>To elucidate whether the difference in fruit size could have an epigenetic basis, whole-genome bisulfite sequencing was performed on samples that were collected at 3 d before pollination (or –3 d after pollination (DAP); when fruits have a similar size and number of cell layers) and at 9 DAP (a few days before observing significant phenotypic differences between the fruits). As expected from their common origin, only a limited number of high-confidence DMRs (<i>n</i> = 197) could be found between young fruits of GDDH13 and GDDH18 at –3 DAP. Of these, 47 DMRs were located within 2 kb upstream of the TSS of genes. Similarly, we identified a total of 148 high-confidence DMRs between fruits of GDDH13 and GDDH18 at 9 DAP. From this analysis, we found that 53 genes contained DMRs in their promoter region (i.e., within 2 kb upstream of the TSS). At both time points a majority of genes with DMRs showed a decrease in methylation in their promoter region for GDDH18 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#MOESM4">Supplementary Table 5</a>). Notably, in both comparisons, DMRs in the CG–CHG and CHG contexts were over-represented.</p><p>The overlap of DMRs between the two time points analyzed included 22 genes with DMRs in their promoter regions, with most of them (<i>n</i> = 17) showing lower methylation in GDDH18 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#MOESM4">Supplementary Table 5</a>). Several of the 22 genes have orthologs in other species with a role that could explain the observed size difference between the GDDH13 and GDDH18 fruits—including SQUAMOSA PROMOTER-BINDING PROTEIN LIKE 13 (<i>SPL13</i>, <i>MD16G0108400</i>), 1-AMINO-CYCLOPROPANE-1-CARBOXYLATE SYNTHASE 8 (<i>ACS8</i>, <i>MD15G0127800</i>) and CYTOCHROME P450 FAMILY 71 SUBFAMILY A POLYPEPTIDE 25 (<i>CYP71A25</i>, <i>MD14G0147300</i>), which belong to the minority of genes with increased methylation in GDDH18.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><p>As a prerequisite to epigenomic studies in apple, we decided to produce a high-quality reference genome for apple. An advantage for us was the availability of the homozygous GDDH13 doubled-haploid line. Assembling a genome that is both highly heterozygous and recently duplicated into a haploid consensus sequence presents a substantial challenge. This is exemplified by the comparison of our first assembly steps to a recently published report on a heterozygous Golden Delicious apple genome sequence<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Li, X. et al. Improved hybrid de novo genome assembly of domesticated apple (Malus × domestica). Gigascience 5, 35 (2016)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d68502e1867">33</a></sup>. Following hybrid assembly of PacBio and Illumina reads, Li and colleagues<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Li, X. et al. Improved hybrid de novo genome assembly of domesticated apple (Malus × domestica). Gigascience 5, 35 (2016)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d68502e1871">33</a></sup> reported a N50 of 112 kb, whereas we obtained a N50 of 620 kb at that same step. These results highlight the power of haploids or doubled haploids in genome sequencing projects<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Zhang, H. et al. Dramatic improvement in genome assembly achieved using doubled-haploid genomes. Sci. Rep. 4, 6780–6785 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d68502e1875">48</a></sup>, particularly in those for apple, which is not only highly heterozygous but has also undergone a recent whole-genome duplication (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Velasco, R. et al. The genome of the domesticated apple (Malus × domestica Borkh.). Nat. Genet. 42, 833–839 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d68502e1878">6</a> and this study). The optical mapping then allowed us to produce scaffolds with a N50 of 5.5 Mb, which, in association with a high-density integrated linkage map, yielded highly contiguous pseudomolecules. In this new apple genome, we followed a newer convention<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Di Pierro, E.A. et al. A high-density, multi-parental SNP genetic map on apple validates a new mapping approach for outcrossing species. Hortic. Res. 3, 16057 (2016)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d68502e1882">23</a></sup> in which the orientation of Chr10 and Chr05 became aligned by the inversion of Chr05. We chose to invert Chr05 because it is the least frequently reported of the two in previous genetic studies on quantitative trait loci (QTL), gene discovery and characterization.</p><p>We estimated the genome size of GDDH13 to be 651 Mb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 2</a>), which suggested that the GDDH13 genome may be smaller than that of the heterozygous Golden Delicious line, which was recently estimated to be 701 Mb (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Li, X. et al. Improved hybrid de novo genome assembly of domesticated apple (Malus × domestica). Gigascience 5, 35 (2016)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d68502e1891">33</a>). Although the GDDH13 tree looks similar to the heterozygous Golden Delicious counterpart (including tree architecture, flowering time and fruit appearance; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#Fig6">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>), it is possible that through the consecutive steps of selfing, haploid development and chromosome doubling, some minor parts of the genome might have been lost or re-arranged. Thus, it is possible that some of the genome sequence might be missing in the GDDH13 assembly.</p><p>Our gene prediction analysis reduced the estimated number of annotated genes in apple from 63,541 (Genome Database for Rosaceae, see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#Sec11">URLs</a> and ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Velasco, R. et al. The genome of the domesticated apple (Malus × domestica Borkh.). Nat. Genet. 42, 833–839 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d68502e1903">6</a>) to 42,140, which is much closer to the 42,812 genes that have been reported for pear<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Wu, J. et al. The genome of the pear (Pyrus bretschneideri Rehd.). Genome Res. 23, 396–408 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d68502e1907">49</a></sup> and the 45,293 genes that were identified after filtering out overlapping genes from the original apple genome annotation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Wu, J. et al. The genome of the pear (Pyrus bretschneideri Rehd.). Genome Res. 23, 396–408 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d68502e1911">49</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>).</p><p>TEs also have an important role in structuring genomes. The in-depth TE annotation we performed showed a major TE burst in apple that we estimated to have happened around 21 Mya. This affected all types of TEs, suggesting that the precursor of the modern apple underwent environmental changes with resulting stresses that led to the activation of these TEs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Belyayev, A. Bursts of transposable elements as an evolutionary driving force. J. Evol. Biol. 27, 2573–2584 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d68502e1921">50</a></sup>. The observed TE burst corresponds to the Miocene epoch (23 Mya to 5 Mya) and may coincide with two events: the divergence between pear and apple<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Zhang, H. et al. Dramatic improvement in genome assembly achieved using doubled-haploid genomes. Sci. Rep. 4, 6780–6785 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d68502e1925">48</a></sup> and an uplift event occurring at the Tian Shan mountains<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Balukhovsky, A.N. &amp; Khain, V.E. Historical Geotectonics—Mesozoic and Cenozoic (CRC Press, 1997)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d68502e1929">51</a></sup>, which cover the region where the ancestor of the apple originates from<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Cornille, A. et al. New insight into the history of domesticated apple: secondary contribution of the European wild apple to the genome of cultivated varieties. PLoS Genet. 8, e1002703 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d68502e1933">52</a></sup>. We hypothesize that these TE bursts, which presumably must have been very different in the predecessor of pear and apple, have contributed to the diversification, and possibly even speciation, of these plants.</p><p>Although our analyses using previously reported approaches<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Melters, D.P. et al. Comparative analysis of tandem repeats from hundreds of species reveals unique insights into centromere evolution. Genome Biol. 14, R10 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d68502e1941">53</a></sup> did not identify any characteristic short centromeric repeat sequence in the apple genome, we can hypothesize the putative localization of centromeres on the GDDH13 chromosomes. We found that the regions in which we observed a decrease in the recombination rate between successive markers of the integrated linkage map coincided with the regions that showed an increase in the estimated level of LD in the core apple collection, as well as an increase in DNA methylation levels. In addition, we identified <i>HODOR</i>, the most repetitive consensus sequence in the apple genome, as being over-represented in these same genomic regions. These findings suggest that centromeric regions in the GDDH13 genome may be located within the regions that show an over-representation of <i>HODOR</i>. Future studies will show whether <i>HODOR</i> has a role in the centromere structure in the apple genome. Blast searches have revealed that the <i>HODOR</i> sequence also exists in pear, and because of its origin from potential horizontal gene transfer events, it will be of great interest to investigate when <i>HODOR</i> first appeared during the Rosaceae evolution.</p><p>The genome-wide distribution of DNA methylation peaked in putative centromeric regions of high LD and high <i>HODOR</i> content. As has been observed in <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Lister, R. et al. Highly integrated single-base resolution maps of the epigenome in Arabidopsis. Cell 133, 523–536 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d68502e1969">43</a></sup>, TEs were enriched and genes strongly depleted for DNA methylation. The 10% of genes that possess high levels of DNA methylation (gene body and surrounding region; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#Fig15">Supplementary Fig. 10</a>), globally showed a very low level of transcription, and these genes may be expressed during very specific developmental stages or tissues. The comparison of the apple leaf and fruit methylomes revealed a noteworthy pattern—the fruit globally had higher CHH DNA methylation levels, which suggested increased activity of the RNA-directed DNA methylation machinery in this organ<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Matzke, M.A., Kanno, T. &amp; Matzke, A.J.M. RNA-directed DNA methylation: the evolution of a complex epigenetic pathway in flowering plants. Annu. Rev. Plant Biol. 66, 243–267 (2015)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d68502e1976">54</a></sup>. Consistent with this observation, it has been shown for <i>Arabidopsis</i> that cell-type-specific DNA methylation differences mainly occur at CHH sites<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Kawakatsu, T. et al. Unique cell-type-specific patterns of DNA methylation in the root meristem. Nat. Plants 2, 16058 (2016)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d68502e1984">55</a></sup>. Notably, DNA methylation differences in the CHH context between leaf and fruit tissues occurred next to 294 genes. Several of these were found to be orthologous to genes that are known to be important regulators of flower and fruit development in other species. This suggests that apple fruit development is regulated by epigenetic processes, which is consistent with data obtained in tomato, demonstrating that DNA methylation is important for fruit ripening<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Manning, K. et al. A naturally occurring epigenetic mutation in a gene encoding an SBP-box transcription factor inhibits tomato fruit ripening. Nat. Genet. 38, 948–952 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d68502e1988">56</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Liu, R. et al. A DEMETER-like DNA demethylase governs tomato fruit ripening. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 112, 10804–10809 (2015)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d68502e1991">57</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Gallusci, P., Hodgman, C., Teyssier, E. &amp; Seymour, G.B. DNA methylation and chromatin regulation during fleshy fruit development and ripening. Front. Plant Sci. 7, 807 (2016)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d68502e1994">58</a></sup>.</p><p>In addition, among the major agronomical traits that contribute to both yield and quality, fruit size is one of the most important for many domesticated crops.
