Two of the key determinants that are known to alter plant organ size are cell number and cell size<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Guo, M. &amp; Simmons, C.R. Cell number counts—the fw2.2 and CNR genes and implications for controlling plant fruit and organ size. Plant Sci. 181, 1–7 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d68502e2001">59</a></sup>. Here we investigated fruit size difference between two isogenic doubled-haploid apple lines. We found that the number of cell layers in the parenchyma of GDDH13 fruits increased more rapidly than those in the parenchyma of the smaller GDDH18 fruits, with significant differences being observed as early as 21 DAP. To identify regulators that contributed to the difference in fruit size between the two doubled-haploid apple lines, we found three genes that potentially contributed to the cell number difference, and these contained DMRs in their promoter regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>).</p><p>The identification of potential molecular mechanisms that control cell-division-related processes by DNA methylation provides new insights into the understanding of this important process. However, by comparing the GDDH13 and GDDH18 genomes, we identified nine SNPs that affect protein sequences, and thus we cannot currently exclude a genetic effect.</p><p>The high-quality reference apple genome sequence reported here offers unprecedented insights into the genome dynamics of a tree and provides an important basis for future studies, not only in apple but also in other Rosaceae species.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Genome assembly of GDDH13.</h3><p><i>Hybrid assembly</i>. The genome assembly was performed using a combination of sequencing technologies: PacBio RS II reads, Illumina paired-end reads (PE) and Illumina mate-pair reads (MP). First, Illumina PE reads were separately assembled using SOAPdevo 2.223 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience 1, 18 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d68502e2027">25</a>). Next, the PacBio reads and Illumina contigs were combined to perform a hybrid assembly using the DBG2OLC pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Ye, C., Hill, C.M., Wu, S., Ruan, J. &amp; Ma, Z.S. DBG2OLC: efficient assembly of large genomes using long erroneous reads of the third-generation sequencing technologies. Sci. Rep. 6, 31900 (2016)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d68502e2031">26</a></sup>.</p><p><i>Assembly polishing</i>. A polishing of the assembly using the Illumina paired-end reads was performed. The 120× Illumina reads were mapped to the contigs using BWA-MEM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Li, H. Toward better understanding of artifacts in variant calling from high-coverage samples. Bioinformatics 30, 2843–2851 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d68502e2040">61</a></sup>. This alignment was then used with Pilon 1.17 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Walker, B.J. et al. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome-assembly improvement. PLoS One 9, e112963 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d68502e2043">62</a>) to correct the assembly.</p><p><i>Mate pair scaffolding</i>. A total of 8.5 Gb of Illumina MP data (approximate sequencing depth = 15×), with an insert size varying between 2 kb and 10 kb, was used to scaffold the assembly. The MP reads were mapped on the corrected contigs using BWA-MEM. The alignments were processed with the BESST<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Sahlin, K., Vezzi, F., Nystedt, B., Lundeberg, J. &amp; Arvestad, L. BESST—efficient scaffolding of large fragmented assemblies. BMC Bioinformatics 15, 281 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d68502e2052">63</a></sup> software to scaffold the assembly.</p><p><i>BioNano scaffolding</i>. A BioNano optical mapping analysis was performed, and data was collected and analyzed with IrisViewer (v2.5). The 397 BioNano maps, with a N50 of 2.649 Mb and a total length of 649.7 Mb, were used in the hybrid assembly step with the scaffolds obtained from the MP scaffolding to assemble the final scaffolds in IrisViewer.</p><p><i>Scaffold validation and anchoring to the genetic map</i>. An integrated multiparental genetic linkage map of apple<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Di Pierro, E.A. et al. A high-density, multi-parental SNP genetic map on apple validates a new mapping approach for outcrossing species. Hortic. Res. 3, 16057 (2016)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d68502e2067">23</a></sup> that was composed of 15,417 SNP markers was used to organize and orientate the scaffolds into chromosome-sized sequences. The probe sequences of the 15,417 markers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Bianco, L. et al. Development and validation of a 20K single-nucleotide polymorphism (SNP) whole-genome genotyping array for apple (Malus × domestica Borkh). PLoS One 9, e110377 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d68502e2071">64</a></sup> were mapped onto the genome using BWA-MEM. The physical and genetic positions of the mapped markers were used to place and orient the scaffolds and contigs relative to each other. Detailed methodological details describing the assembly processes can be found in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>.</p><p><i>Linkage disequilibrium (LD)</i>. The 'Old Dessert' INRA core collection, comprising 278 accessions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Lassois, L. et al. Genetic diversity, population structure, parentage analysis and construction of core collections in the French apple germplasm based on SSR markers. Plant Mol. Biol. Rep. 34, 827–844 (2016)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d68502e2083">27</a></sup>, was genotyped with the Axiom Apple-480K SNP genotyping array<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Bianco, L. et al. Development and validation of the Axiom Apple480K SNP genotyping array. Plant J. 86, 62–74 (2016)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d68502e2087">28</a></sup>. LD was estimated with the <i>r</i><sup>2</sup> statistics using the R package snpStats (R package version 1.16.0). Heat maps of pairwise LD between markers were plotted using the R package LDheatmap<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Shin, J.H., Blay, S., McNeney, B. &amp; Graham, J. LDheatmap: an R function for graphical display of pairwise linkage disequilibria between single-nucleotide polymorphisms. J. Stat. Software 16, c03 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d68502e2095">65</a></sup>.</p><p><i>RNA sequencing (RNA-seq) analysis</i>. To maximize the number and diversity of genes that were identified by RNA-seq, mRNA was purified from various organs at multiple developmental stages derived from seven cultivars and hybrids. A total of nine libraries were generated (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a> for more details).</p><p>The cDNA sequencing libraries were constructed following the manufacturer's instructions (Illumina, San Diego, CA, USA), and the Illumina GA processing pipeline Cassava 1.7.0 was used for image analysis and base-calling.</p><p><i>DNA extraction from leaf and developing fruits, and bisulfite sequencing</i>. Young leaves from GDDH13 and developing fruits from GDDH13 and GDDH18 (two biological replicates from independently grafted trees) were collected 3 d before pollination (–3 DAP, with petals, sepals, anthers and styles removed) and 9 DAP. DNA was purified using the Macherey-Nagel NucleoSpin plant II DNA extraction kit (Germany), following the manufacturer's instructions. Bisulfite treatment was applied to determine the cytosine methylation status, using the Epitect bisulfite kit (Qiagen) and 100 ng of genomic DNA.</p><p>Whole-genome bisulfite sequencing was performed, and DMRs between leaves and young GDDH13 fruits, and between GDDH13 and GDDH18 fruits, at –3 DAP and 9 DAP were computed according to Hagmann <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Hagmann, J. et al. Century-scale methylome stability in a recently diverged Arabidopsis thaliana lineage. PLoS Genet. 11, e1004920 (2015)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d68502e2121">47</a></sup>. DNA methylation distribution plots and gene clustering analyses by methylation patterns were performed with deepTools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Ramírez, F., Dündar, F., Diehl, S., Grüning, B.A. &amp; Manke, T. deepTools: a flexible platform for exploring deep-sequencing data. Nucleic Acids Res. 42, W187–W191 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d68502e2125">66</a></sup>.</p><p><i>Small RNA alignment</i>. Apple sRNA sequences derived from mature fruit parenchyma<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Celton, J.M. et al. Widespread antisense transcription in apple is correlated with siRNA production and indicates a large potential for transcriptional and/or post-transcriptional control. New Phytol. 203, 287–299 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d68502e2135">35</a></sup> were aligned to the Golden Delicious doubled-haploid pseudomolecules using BWA-MEM. Only perfectly mapped sequences were considered further, and reads with identical sequences were allowed to be mapped to two or more loci.</p><p><i>Genome annotation</i>. RNA-seq data derived from nine different libraries was <i>de novo</i> assembled using Trinity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Grabherr, M.G. et al. Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-seq data without a reference genome. Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 644–652 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d68502e2147">67</a></sup> and SOAPdenovo-trans<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Xie, Y. et al. SOAPdenovo-Trans: de novo transcriptome assembly with short RNA-seq reads. Bioinformatics 30, 1660–1666 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d68502e2151">68</a></sup>. For each library, the assembly with the highest N50 value was chosen to annotate the genes. 2,033 mRNAs and 326,941 expressed sequence tags (ESTs) extracted from the NCBI nucleotide and EST databases, respectively, were also used for gene prediction.</p><p>The structural annotation of coding genes was performed using EuGene<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Foissac, S. et al. Genome annotation in plants and fungi: EuGene as a model platform. Curr. Bioinform. 3, 87–97 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d68502e2158">32</a></sup> by combining Gmap transcript mapping<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Wu, T.D. &amp; Watanabe, C.K. GMAP: a genomic mapping and alignment program for mRNA and EST sequences. Bioinformatics 21, 1859–1875 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d68502e2162">69</a></sup>, similarities detected with plant proteomes and Swiss-Prot, and <i>ab initio</i> predictions (interpolated Marlov model and weight-array matrix for donor and acceptor splicing sites). Moreover, the EuGene prediction was completed by tRNAscan-SE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Lowe, T.M. &amp; Chan, P.P. tRNAscan-SE On-line: integrating search and context for analysis of transfer RNA genes. Nucleic Acids Res. 44, W54–W57 (2016)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d68502e2169">70</a></sup>, RNAmmer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Lagesen, K. et al. RNAmmer: consistent and rapid annotation of ribosomal RNA genes. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, 3100–3108 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d68502e2173">71</a></sup> and RfamScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Nawrocki, E.P. et al. Rfam 12.0: updates to the RNA families database. Nucleic Acids Res. 43 (W1), D130–D137 (2015)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d68502e2178">72</a></sup> to annotate non-protein-coding genes, including those encoding tRNA, rRNA, miRNA and snoRNA, and other regions with proof of transcription but without significant similarities and coding potential (named ncRNA).</p><p>Functional annotation of proteins was performed using InterProScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Jones, P. et al. InterProScan 5: genome-scale protein function classification. Bioinformatics 30, 1236–1240 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d68502e2185">73</a></sup>. The functional annotation was then completed by the prediction of targeted signals using the TargetP software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Emanuelsson, O., Brunak, S., von Heijne, G. &amp; Nielsen, H. Locating proteins in the cell using TargetP, SignalP and related tools. Nat. Protoc. 2, 953–971 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d68502e2189">74</a></sup>.</p><p><i>Genome synteny</i>. SynMap (CoGe, see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#Sec11">URLs</a>) was used to identify collinearity blocks using homologous coding sequence pairs. Detailed methodological details on the annotation processes can be found in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>.</p><p><i>Comparison of annotation between the heterozygous Golden Delicious and GDDH13 genomes</i>. <i>Malus domestica</i> predicted gene (MDP) sequences obtained from the heterozygous genome annotation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Velasco, R. et al. The genome of the domesticated apple (Malus × domestica Borkh.). Nat. Genet. 42, 833–839 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d68502e2212">6</a></sup> were mapped to the GDDH13 genome assembly using the best BLAT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Kent, W.J. BLAT—the BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Res. 12, 656–664 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d68502e2216">75</a></sup> hit. Comparison of the two genome annotations was done using Bio++<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Guéguen, L. et al. Bio++: efficient extensible libraries and tools for computational molecular evolution. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 1745–1750 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d68502e2220">76</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Repeat annotation.</h3><p>The TE<i>denovo</i> pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Flutre, T., Duprat, E., Feuillet, C. &amp; Quesneville, H. Considering transposable element diversification in de novo annotation approaches. PLoS One 6, e16526 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d68502e2235">37</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Hoede, C. et al. PASTEC: an automatic transposable element classification tool. PLoS One 9, e91929 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d68502e2238">77</a></sup> from the REPET package v2.5 (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#Sec11">URLs</a>) was used to detect TEs in genomic sequences and to provide a consensus sequence for each TE family. Consensus TE sequences were used to annotate the TE copies in the whole genome using the TEannot pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Quesneville, H. et al. Combined evidence annotation of transposable elements in genome sequences. PLOS Comput. Biol. 1, 166–175 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d68502e2245">38</a></sup> from the REPET package v2.5. Consensus sequences that were classified as potential host genes because they contain host gene Pfam domains were kept from this study. The same process was used to identify the <i>HODOR</i> consensus sequence on the PacBio assembly with the REPET pipeline. TE insertion ages were calculated using the adapted <i>T</i> = <i>K</i>/<i>r</i> formula for nonduplicated LTR sequences, where <i>K</i> is the sequence divergence, and <i>r</i> is the substitution rate<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="de la Chaux, N., Tsuchimatsu, T., Shimizu, K.K. &amp; Wagner, A. The predominantly selfing plant Arabidopsis thaliana experienced a recent reduction in transposable element abundance compared to its outcrossing relative Arabidopsis lyrata. Mob. DNA 3, 2 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d68502e2268">78</a></sup>. The observed sequence divergence was corrected with the Jukes and Cantor model<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Jukes, T.H. &amp; Cantor, C.R. in Mammalian Protein Metabolism (ed. Munro, H.N.) 21–132 (Elsevier, 1969)." href="/articles/ng.3886#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d68502e2273">79</a></sup>. Additional methodological details on the repeat annotation can be found in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Data availability.</h3><p>This whole-genome shotgun project has been deposited at GenBank under the accession code <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=MJAX00000000.1">MJAX00000000.1</a>. The raw Illumina mRNA sequences were submitted to the NCBI under BioProject ID <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA191060">PRJNA191060</a>, and the GDDH18 genome reads were deposited under BioProject ID <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA379390">PRJNA379390</a>. DNA methylation data can be accessed on the Gene Expression Omnibus website under accession codes <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/geo/query/acc.cgi?acc=GSE87014">GSE87014</a> and <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/geo/query/acc.cgi?acc=GSE93950">GSE93950</a>. Structural and functional annotations are available through our genome browser (<a href="https://iris.angers.inra.fr/gddh13/">https://iris.angers.inra.fr/gddh13/</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">URLs.</h3><p>Structural and functional annotations are available through our genome browser: <a href="https://iris.angers.inra.fr/gddh13/">https://iris.angers.inra.fr/gddh13/</a>.</p><p>The Whole-Genome Shotgun project can be found in GenBank under: <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/MJAX00000000.1">https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/MJAX00000000.1</a>.</p><p>The REPET package v2.5 used to detect TEs used in this study can be found here: <a href="https://urgi.versailles.inra.fr/Tools/REPET">https://urgi.versailles.inra.fr/Tools/REPET</a></p><p>SynMap- CoGe: <a href="http://www.genomevolution.org">http://www.genomevolution.org</a></p><p>Genome Database for Rosaceae: <a href="http://www.rosaceae.org">http://www.rosaceae.org</a>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec16" data-title="Additional information"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec16-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec16">Additional information</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec16-content"><p><b>Publisher's note:</b> Springer Nature remains neutral with regard to jurisdictional claims in published maps and institutional affiliations.</p></div></div></section>

E.B., E.S., J.-M.C., R.V. and H.v.d.G. supported and performed sequencing and genome-mapping experiments; N.D., E.S., J.-M.C., G.L., L.B. and H.v.d.G. performed genome assemblies; J.-M.C. performed field and wet lab work; G.L. and L.B. performed k-mer spectra analysis and genome size estimations; G.L., L.B., D.M., R.V., E.A.D.P., H.M., C.-E.D., F.L., E.v.d.W. and M.T. provided genetic map information and performed quality control experiments; M.T. and E.A.D.P. made the plots comparing genetic and physical maps and performed recombination rate analyses; H.M. provided LD plots; D.M. and M.T. made the haplotype map plot; N.D., S.A., S.G. and J.G. performed gene annotation; E.B. and N.D. performed genome duplication analysis; N.C., H.Q., E.B. and S.A. annotated and analyzed TEs; C.B. performed bisulfite sequencing; C.B., J.-M.C., N.D., D.W. and E.B. analyzed bisulfite sequencing results; J.-M.C. and D.J.G.R. provided RNA-seq data; Y.L. created the GDDH13 and GDDH18 lines, with support from P.G.; S.G. set up the genome browser; J.-M.C., N.D. and E.B. wrote and edited most of the manuscript; and all authors read and commented on the manuscript.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="corresponding-author">Corresponding author</h3><p id="corresponding-author-list">Correspondence to

            </div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec17" data-title="Integrated supplementary information"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec17-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec17">Integrated supplementary information</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec17-content"><div data-test="supplementary-info"><div id="figshareContainer" class="c-article-figshare-container" data-test="figshare-container"></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig6"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/ng.3886/figures/6" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3886/MediaObjects/41588_2017_Article_BFng3886_Fig6_ESM.jpg">Supplementary Figure 1 Breeding the doubled-haploid GDDH13.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>(A) In 1963 the 'Golden Delicious' variety was selfed once resulting in P21R1A50 (confirmed by isoenzymatic analysis). This line was then self-pollinated in 1986 resulting in a haploid plant from an unfertilized egg cell rather than a zygote in 1987. Samples of this haploid line were put <i>in vitro</i>, which resulted in spontaneous doubling events (1988, indicated by more vigorous growth). Root formation was induced <i>in vitro</i> and the plants were transferred to the orchard on their own roots resulting in GDDH13 (X9273). GDDH13 was then grafted in 2003. (B) and (C) show photographs of flowers and a fruit of GDDH13.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig7"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/ng.3886/figures/7" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3886/MediaObjects/41588_2017_Article_BFng3886_Fig7_ESM.jpg">Supplementary Figure 2 Comparison of the genetic and physical GDDH13 position of the SNP markers of the integrated genetic map.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>Graphical representation of the location of Single Nucleotide Polymorphism (SNP) markers on the physical map (x axis) compared to their position on the integrated genetic map (y axis) for all chromosomes. Each marker is plotted in the color of the chromosome to which it has been genetically mapped to. The colors correspond to Fig. 2 where syntenic chromosomes are depicted in similar color tones.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig8"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/ng.3886/figures/8" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3886/MediaObjects/41588_2017_Article_BFng3886_Fig8_ESM.jpg">Supplementary Figure 3 Comparison of the genetic and physical genome v.1 position of the markers of the integrated genetic map.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>Like <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3886#Fig7">Suppl. Fig. 2</a> but using the previous genome release (v.1). All markers mapping to the correct chromosome are plotted in green, the ones to the wrong chromosome in red. This graph is shown to visualize the overall improvement that has been achieved by the new sequencing effort.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig9"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/ng.3886/figures/9" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3886/MediaObjects/41588_2017_Article_BFng3886_Fig9_ESM.jpg">Supplementary Figure 4 Linkage disequilibrium analysis for all GDDH13 chromosomes.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>Heatmap of genotypic linkage disequilibrium (LD, r<sup>2</sup>) in all GDDH13 chromosomes in the 'Old Dessert' INRA apple core collection. The top part of the figure is a graphical representation of the location of single nucleotide polymorphism markers (SNPs) on the physical map (top) with correspondence to their order in a regular distribution (bottom). The color coding represents high LD (red) and low LD (blue).</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig10"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/ng.3886/figures/10" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3886/MediaObjects/41588_2017_Article_BFng3886_Fig10_ESM.jpg">Supplementary Figure 5 Haplotype map of GDDH13.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>The genetic phase obtained through the construction of a genetic map from a cross between Golden Delicious and Renetta Grigia di Torriana was used to depict the two haplotypes of Golden Delicious in the GDDH13 assembly. The blue and red color represents the two different alleles of the heterozygous markers of Golden Delicious.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig11"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/ng.3886/figures/11" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3886/MediaObjects/41588_2017_Article_BFng3886_Fig11_ESM.jpg">Supplementary Figure 6 Histogram showing the number of mapped sRNAs of 21, 22, 23 and 24 nucleotides on genes and transposable elements of the GDDH13 genome.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>Insert figure caption here by deleting or overwriting this text; captions may run to a second page if necessary. (B) log2 expression values of <i>HODOR</i> in different tissues.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig14"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/ng.3886/figures/14" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3886/MediaObjects/41588_2017_Article_BFng3886_Fig14_ESM.jpg">Supplementary Figure 9 Genomic DNA methylation density in GDDH13.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>DNA methylation ratios were plotted for all chromosomes (including Chr00) and the three DNA sequence contexts: CG in brown, CHG in yellow and CHH in blue.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig15"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/ng.3886/figures/15" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3886/MediaObjects/41588_2017_Article_BFng3886_Fig15_ESM.jpg">Supplementary Figure 10 DNA methylation distribution on genes in apple.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>Three main clusters of genes presenting different DNA methylation distributions over their body and surrounding sequences were detected. Cluster 1 includes genes rich in DNA methylation in their body, upstream and downstream of their transcription start site (TSS) and transcription end site (TES) respectively. Cluster 2 genes are located in genomic regions enriched in DNA methylation and cluster 3 genes are located in regions with less overall DNA methylation. The images at the bottom represent the level of DNA methylation for each gene of a cluster along its coding sequence, with a color coding, indicated at the right of the images, from low (red) to high (blue).</p></div></div></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec18" data-title="Supplementary information"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec18-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec18">Supplementary information</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec18-content"><div data-test="supplementary-info">
                  
                <div class="c-article-supplementary__item" data-test="supp-item" id="MOESM1"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="https://static-content.springer.com/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3886/MediaObjects/41588_2017_BFng3886_MOESM1_ESM.pdf" data-supp-info-image="">Supplementary Text and Figures</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>Supplementary Figures 1–10, Supplementary Tables 1–2 and Supplementary Note (PDF 2650 kb)</p></div></div>
